        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202403321')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="886fb40905dd4c0094309a4e166dece8">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'dbbaf2fb-df59-42e9-bcb2-3c88d8153932'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d198278670" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal derivatives exhibit high theoretical capacity, making them promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Sulfides, known for their superior electrical conductivity compared to oxides, enhance charge transfer, leading to improved electrochemical performance. Here, a hierarchical WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower is synthesized by thermal sulfurization of WO<sub>3</sub>. Comprising interconnected thin nanosheets, this structure offers increased surface area, facilitating extensive internal surfaces for electrochemical redox reactions. The WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower demonstrates a specific capacity of ≈334&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, nearly three times higher than its oxide counterpart. Further, it shows very stable performance as a high-temperature (65&nbsp;°C) anode with ≈180&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> reversible capacity at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate. Post-cycling analysis confirms unchanged morphology, highlighting the structural stability and robustness of WS<sub>2</sub>. DFT calculations show that the electronic bandgap in both WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> increases when going from the bulk to monolayers. Na adsorption calculations show that Na atoms bind strongly in WO<sub>3</sub> with a higher energy diffusion barrier when compared to WS<sub>2</sub>, corroborating the experimental findings. This study presents a significant insight into electrode material selection for sodium-ion storage applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202403321-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202403321-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Due to the rapid depletion of fossil energies, there's a critical need for indispensable energy storehouse systems. In this search, battery technology became the first choice due to its energy density and cycling stability. To date, lithium-ion battery (LIB) is the most used rechargeable battery technology, but people are seeking an indispensable choice due to the high cost of lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0001_R_d198278662e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this script, sodium-ion battery (NIB) technology is considered the most promising, indispensable choice due to its low cost, terrain-friendly, and naturally abundant worldwide.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0002_R_d198278662e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mater. Today Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Presently, NIB will be commercialized veritably soon. Still, suitable host materials will give a reasonable electrochemical performance with satisfying energy density and less volume expansion during the nonstop sodiation/de-sodiation process.</p>
         
         <p>The choice of NIB anode depends on various factors: electrochemical stability, cost, cornucopia, etc. In this hunt, transition essence sulfide would be suitable due to multielectron transfer and high theoretical capacity as NIB anode concerning their oxide counterpart. (i) The sulfide accoutrements give advanced ionic conductivity than their oxide part, enhancing fast Na<sup>+</sup>-ion prolixity and performing better electrochemical performances at the advanced current rate. (iii) The energy difference between O 2p and S 2p levels would facilitate the higher electrical conductivity of sulfides than corresponding oxides, facilitating the charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0003_R_d198278662e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (iii) Sulfide-grounded accouterments allow easy access of Na<sup>+</sup>-ion during the sodiation/de-sodiation process as it has advanced interlayer distance compared to oxide counterparts, flaunting advanced capacity with bettered cycling stability as NIB anode. (iv) Sulfide-based materials follow alloying reactions with Na<sup>+</sup>-ion, contributing to higher electrochemical performance resulting in a better battery anode. (v) The sulfides exhibit a more stable flexible structure than their oxide counterpart due to a lower electronegativity of S than O, resulting in reversible electrochemical reactions with Na<sup>+</sup>-ions and improved electrochemical stability over multiple charge/discharge cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0004_R_d198278662e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Chem. Front.">4</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0005_R_d198278662e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Technol.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These properties of sulfides help to prevent the cracking of the electrode, pulverization, and loss of active material, which prevents the capacity fading over multiple cycles. In this scenario, several binary and ternary transition metal sulfides are widely reported as lithium-ion battery anodes and electrochemical supercapacitors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0004_R_d198278662e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Chem. Front.">4</a>-<a href="#smll202403321-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0006_R_d198278662e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electrochim. Acta">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, various 2D binary transition metal sulfide, e.g; MoS<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>2</sub>S, FeS, VS<sub>2</sub>, TiS<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, etc. based materials are also used as NIB anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0007_R_d198278662e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Phys Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0008_R_d198278662e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It reveals the average specific capacity of ≈150–450&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0008_R_d198278662e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Still, there is no report on the in-depth analysis between the metal-sulfide and corresponding metal-oxide comparison and why the sulfide has better electrochemical performances as a battery anode. To understand this mechanism, we have chosen a binary 2D transition metal WS<sub>2</sub> (tungsten disulfide) and its corresponding oxide WO<sub>3</sub> (tungsten oxide) and characterized it as a NIB anode. The inner layer S–W–S is bonded by a strong covalent bond, but the interlayers are connected through a weak van der Waals force, having 0.6&nbsp;nm interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This special structural characteristics feature accelerates fast ion transfer through the 2D matrix. Due to having that advantage 2D-WS<sub>2</sub> has gained attention for NIB applications.</p>
         
         <p>Only a couple of reports are available on WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> as NIB anode. For instance, Yan et&nbsp;al. reported WS<sub>2</sub> nanofibers as NIB anode with 395&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> specific capacity at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0010_R_d198278662e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Electrochim. Acta">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xu et&nbsp;al. showed WS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as NIB anode ≈360&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0011_R_d198278662e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while 3D carbon foam supported WS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets shows 268.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0012_R_d198278662e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Grapne@WS<sub>2</sub> composite delivered ≈450&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> specific capacity at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0013_R_d198278662e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The WO<sub>3</sub> nanomaterials delivered ≈120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 250&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate reported by Aparicio et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0014_R_d198278662e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Electrochim. Acta">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus the WS<sub>2</sub> has been demonstrated as a promising NIB anode material with high specific theoretical capacity. Based on the previous report, integrating WS<sub>2</sub> in a conductive carbon matrix alleviated volume change during the charge/discharge process and also enhanced electronic conductivity resulting in superior electrochemical performances.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0012_R_d198278662e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0013_R_d198278662e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the poor tap density of the conductive carbon matrix would significantly limit the volumetric energy density of the final device. Moreover, there is no report about the electrochemical performance of WS<sub>2</sub> at elevated temperatures. Hence there is still a lot of scope to improve the carbon-free NIB anode performance in terms of long-term cycling stability by mitigating structural change and admirable performance beyond room temperature. Hence, further research needs to be carried out on optimizing WS<sub>2</sub> as a carbon-free NIB anode material having excellent long-term cycling performance at a higher current density, the ability to function at high temperatures and in-depth theoretical analysis.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we aimed to investigate the optimal strategy for sodium storage and comprehend the reaction mechanisms between sulfide and oxide materials. A comprehensive analysis was conducted, both experimentally and theoretically, comparing WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flowers. When utilized as NIB anodes, the carbon-free WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower exhibited an impressive reversible capacity of 334&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Conversely, the WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flower only achieved a capacity of 102&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under the same conditions. Notably, the WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower displayed remarkable results, maintaining a reversible capacity of 211&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 500 cycles, retaining ≈98.9% of its capacity. Furthermore, under elevated temperature conditions (65&nbsp;°C) and at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower demonstrated a stable specific capacity of ≈180&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, we have carried out ab-initio calculations based on Density Functional Theory (DFT) to elucidate the superior electrochemical performance of WS<sub>2</sub> in comparison to WO<sub>3</sub> by investigating the electronic properties of bulk and monolayer counterparts of Tungsten sulfide and oxide as well as their interaction with Na atoms.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202403321-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202403321-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403321-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0001">1a</a> displays a typical XRD spectrum of the as-synthesized WO<sub>3</sub>. All peaks in this spectrum can be accurately matched to an Orthorhombic phase of WO<sub>3</sub>. The estimated lattice constants a = 7.38&nbsp;Å, b = 7.512&nbsp;Å, and c = 3.84&nbsp;Å, with α = β = γ = 90° and a volume of 213.33&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup> (JCPDS card # 020–1324), align well with the reported crystal structure of WO<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0001">1b</a>). The sharpness of the diffraction peaks indicates excellent crystallization of the as-synthesized WO<sub>3</sub>. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0001">1c</a>, the peaks correspond well to hexagonal WS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 01-087-2417). The calculated lattice parameters are a = b = 3.18&nbsp;Å, c = 12.98&nbsp;Å, with α = β = 90° and γ = 120.0°, and a volume of 113.96&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup>, as depicted in the crystallographic image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0001">1d</a>. The absence of additional WO<sub>3</sub> diffraction peaks confirms the formation of pure WS<sub>2</sub>. The XRD patterns for both WO<sub>3</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> demonstrate the phase purity of the sample.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ede3cba-ece5-42f9-884f-633122da0856/smll202403321-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ede3cba-ece5-42f9-884f-633122da0856/smll202403321-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2021e522-b4e2-4f99-b4e2-564a1bbb8dff/smll202403321-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ede3cba-ece5-42f9-884f-633122da0856/smll202403321-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern with the corresponding JCPDS patterns for orthorhombic WO<sub>3</sub> b) crystal structures of WO<sub>3</sub> c) XRD pattern with the corresponding JCPDS patterns for hexagonal WO<sub>3</sub> d) and crystal structures of WS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The chemical composition and oxidation states of the elements were measured using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2</a>. The survey scan (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2a</a>) of WO<sub>3</sub> sample shows the presence of W, O, and C. The core level W 4f (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2b</a>) spectra is effectively resolved into the tungsten W 4f<sub>5/2</sub> and W 4f<sub>7/2</sub> orbitals, with binding energies of 37.32 and 35.20&nbsp;eV, respectively. This observation confirms the occurrence of the W<sup>6+</sup> oxidation state in WO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0015_R_d198278662e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Non-Oxide Glas.">15</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0016_R_d198278662e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochim. Acta">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding energy difference of 2.1&nbsp;eV between the W 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and W 4f<sub>5/2</sub> core levels also suggests that tungsten (W) in tungsten trioxide (WO<sub>3</sub>) is in a +6-oxidation state. The deconvoluted spectra of the O 1s core level are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2c</a>. The XPS survey scan of WS<sub>2</sub> sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2d</a>) clearly shows the binding energy peaks for tungsten (W), sulfur (S), oxygen (O), and carbon (C). In the W 4f spectrum, the binding energies for the 4 f<sub>7/2</sub>,4f<sub>5/2</sub>, and 5 p<sub>3/2</sub>, peaks are at 33.50, 35.00, and 39.50&nbsp;eV, respectively which confirms the tetravalency of W (W<sup>4+</sup>), and for S 2p spectrum, 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks at 162.10 and 163.50&nbsp;eV are confirming the presence of S<sup>2−</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2e,f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0017_R_d198278662e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Technol.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0018_R_d198278662e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Technol.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e1cbff67-ce5a-42d2-a609-03369826d517/smll202403321-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e1cbff67-ce5a-42d2-a609-03369826d517/smll202403321-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03cbe0b0-7398-4701-85d3-58281516745f/smll202403321-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e1cbff67-ce5a-42d2-a609-03369826d517/smll202403321-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS survey spectrum of a) WO<sub>3</sub> b) W4f, c) O1s correcponds to WO<sub>3</sub>, and d) survey spectrum of WS<sub>2</sub>, e) W4f, f) S2p of WS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The high-resolution scanning electron microscopy (HRSEM) image for WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0003">3a</a>), illustrates the micro-flower-like morphology present in both samples, characterized by interconnected nanosheets. These nanosheets collectively form a hierarchical structure within the micro-flower, exhibiting a diameter ranging from 1.0 to 1.5&nbsp;µm. The layered arrangement resembling a sheet within the microstructure notably amplifies the surface area, thereby facilitating ion passage. The distinctive hyper-branched floral pattern of these nanosheets emerges due to successive nucleation events. However, this unique structure remarkably enhances the contact area between the electrode and electrolyte, thereby promoting efficient Na<sup>+</sup>-ion diffusion pathways. This feature contributes significantly to higher reversible capacities and stability, especially during high current rate cycling performance. The interconnected nature of these nanosheets fosters the creation of numerous ionic channels within the electrolyte, establishing extensive internal surfaces essential for an effective electrochemical redox reaction. Consequently, this structural characteristic significantly augments the specific capacity observed in the material.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fab3fe41-b46a-4963-acdb-d79b7c42c5c8/smll202403321-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fab3fe41-b46a-4963-acdb-d79b7c42c5c8/smll202403321-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2ef666c8-7fd7-49ba-98e8-54499a7abde7/smll202403321-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fab3fe41-b46a-4963-acdb-d79b7c42c5c8/smll202403321-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRSEM image of a) WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower b) TEM image of a single fragment of WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower in high resolution c) HRTEM image of fringes with SAED inset d) HRSEM image of WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flower e) TEM image of a single fragment of WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flower in high resolution f) HRTEM image of WO<sub>3</sub> fringes with SAED inset.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image of the WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0003">3b</a>) distinctly reveals the presence of thin, transparent nanosheets situated on the surface of the micro-sphere. The images also display fragments of the microsphere, evident from visible fractures likely occurring during the sample preparation via ultrasonication. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0003">3c</a> presents a high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image, highlighting a lattice spacing of 0.26&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (002) plane of Hexagonal WS<sub>2</sub>. A similar microstructure is confirmed by HRSEM and TEM images for the WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flower (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0003">3d,e</a>). Additionally, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0003">3f</a> showcases lattice spacings of 0.38 and 0.26&nbsp;nm, which correspond to the (001) and (220) planes of WO<sub>3</sub>, respectively. These spacings align consistently with the values obtained from X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403321-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Characterization as Room Temperature NIB Anode</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical sodium storage properties of WO<sub>3</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower electrodes are evaluated by CV measurements. The CV of WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower in the voltage range of 0.001–3.0&nbsp;V was done at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> scan rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The CV shows there is a very negligible capacity contribution after 2.5&nbsp;V voltage, and it is expected to develop the anode material in the lower voltage region. Then we reduced the voltage window to 0.001–2.5&nbsp;V. The CV measurements were done for WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flower at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> scan rate for the first three consecutive cycles, shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, respectively. During the first cathodic scan, there was a sharp peak at 0.61&nbsp;V, indicating Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion into WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower lattice, through the conversion reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other reduction peak observed at 0.15&nbsp;V is due to the formation of W and Na<sub>2</sub>S and SEI layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the anodic scan peaks at 0.37, and 1.37&nbsp;V were observed, which indicates the oxidation of W (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202403321-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)). Another sharp peak was observed at 1.98&nbsp;V, which corresponds reversible conversion reaction between W<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202403321-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0019_R_d198278662e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0020_R_d198278662e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloys Compd.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The redox peaks overlapped from the second cycle onward, indicating reversible reaction and stability. The CV corresponds to the following probable reactions for WS<sub>2</sub> as NIB anode:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0021_R_d198278662e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochim. Acta">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/face057a-3096-40c0-8043-62654516f153/smll202403321-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/face057a-3096-40c0-8043-62654516f153/smll202403321-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3ac829c2-0b96-49a0-8c0f-e65fbced2874/smll202403321-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/face057a-3096-40c0-8043-62654516f153/smll202403321-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cyclic Voltammetry a) WS<sub>2</sub>, b) WO<sub>3</sub> in a potential window of 0.001–2.5&nbsp;V, c) charge–discharge profiles at a current density of 15&nbsp;mAg<sup>−1</sup> for 2nd cycle, cycling performance of d) WO<sub>3</sub> and e) WS<sub>2</sub> as 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate, long cycling at a current density of f) 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and g) 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate for WS<sub>2</sub>, h) rate performance of WS<sub>2</sub> at a current density of 15–2000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> current rate.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The CV for WO<sub>3</sub> was done at 0.01–2.5&nbsp;V voltage window for the first three consecutive cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4b</a>). The peak observed at a potential of 1.51 and 1.16&nbsp;V during the initial reduction process can be attributed to the production of the nonstoichiometric Na<sub>2</sub>WO<sub>3</sub> phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4b</a>). This phenomenon is believed to occur due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the interlayers of WO<sub>3</sub>. The 3rd peak observed at a potential of 0.39&nbsp;V can be attributed to the development of the solid-state interphase (SEI). On the other hand, the peak observed at 0.40&nbsp;V corresponds to the subsequent insertion of sodium ions (Na<sup>+</sup>) to generate metallic tungsten (W) and sodium oxide (Na<sub>2</sub>O).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0022_R_d198278662e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following oxidation process, the two distinct peaks observed at 1.54 and 2.13&nbsp;V are attributed to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, as well as the conversion of W<sup>0</sup> to WO<sub>3</sub> (as shown in Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202403321-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>)). Starting from the second cycle onward, it is observed that the reduction and oxidation peaks are localized at the potentials of 2.13 and 0.39&nbsp;V, respectively. These potentials correspond to the conversion reactions occurring between WO<sub>3</sub> and W. In addition, it can be observed that the CVs exhibit significant overlap, from the second cycle onward. It suggests a high degree of reversibility for tungsten trioxide WO<sub>3</sub>. The overall electrochemical reaction between WO<sub>3</sub> and Na-ion is given in Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202403321-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>).

               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403321-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6b100d17-a566-441d-8aa1-c0bf12d5605f/smll202403321-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403321:smll202403321-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403321-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">W</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>6</mn>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>6</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">W</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{W}}{{{\mathrm{O}}}_3} + 6{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + } + 6{{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - } \to 3{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_2}{\mathrm{O}} + {\mathrm{W}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The second cycle GCD profiles of WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> micro-flower were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4c</a> at 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current density. The charge/discharge capacity were shown 372/418 and 203/216&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4c</a>). The plateaus of the GCD plots were perfectly overlapped with the CV curves for both cases, indicating the reversibility of the cell.</p>
            
            <p>Now, the cycling stability tests were performed at 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current density for both the samples (WO<sub>3</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub>), it is clearly observed that the initial specific capacity of WO<sub>3</sub> electrode is 225&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> which got faded after a few cycles, and maintained up to ≈91&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4d</a>), whereas in case of WS<sub>2</sub> keeping the same parameters the initial specific capacity to be 417&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and without much fading it maintained up to ≈336&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4e</a>). This elevated specific capacity by 3.5 times makes WS<sub>2</sub> a promising material for sodium storage applications. We have shown GCD plots for WO<sub>3</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. It shows the curves overlap very well with increasing the cycle number, which corresponds to the CV plots for both cases. It indicates the reversibility of the electrode materials for both WO<sub>3</sub> and WS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. As the electrochemical performance at high current is crucial for practical application, WS<sub>2</sub> is tested in higher current density of 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> up to 200 cycles which shows an initial specific capacity of ≈400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and after initial few cycles fading got stable to a specific capacity of ≈257&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating ≈64% capacity retention after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4f</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, long-term cycling stability was performed at a high current density of 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4g</a>). As we know, initially, for a couple of cycles, the electrode is not activated; that's why the polarization of the cell is relatively high.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0023_R_d198278662e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Phys. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reversible capacity was decreasing. This kind of phenomenon is very common for battery electrode materials. After a couple of cycles later, once the cell gets activated, the polarizations become negligible, and the reversible capacity becomes stable. It showed a stable specific capacity of ≈212&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at the 500th cycle, with outstanding capacity restoration property. The coulombic efficiency, even after 500 cycles, was ≈99%, which reveals the reversibility of the electrode. It was almost stable after slight capacity decay after the initial couple of cycles.</p>
            
            <p>The rate performance of any electrode material is a very important property. The rate capability of the 2D WS<sub>2</sub> electrode was measured at various current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4h</a>). Initially, the electrode shows a reversible capacity of ≈366&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate, and when the current rate increased to 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> it shows ≈273&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> reversible capacity, which reveals ≈74.5% of the retention of capacity. Further, the current density increased by 50 to 500, 1000, and 2000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The reversible capacity still shows ≈241, ≈172, and ≈108&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which implies extraordinary capacity restoration property. After that, the cell was moved back to its initial current density from 1000 to 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the cell still showed ≈173 and ≈335&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> reversible capacity, which reveals ≈100%, and ≈91.5% capacity restoration, with respect to its initial capacity. This result shows outstanding cycling stability of WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower without any carbon additive composite. Further, the superiority of our developed WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower as NIB anode was compared with previously reported literature and presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">It is very important to understand the reaction kinetics of the electrode materials. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0005">5a–c</a> exhibits the kinetic analysis of WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower to explain the capacitive and diffusive contribution to the total capacity values. The CVs were done at different scan rates, 0.3, 0.5, and 0.75&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0005">5a</a>). As the scan rate increases, the current is also increased, which is very common for any electrochemical measurements. The increased peak currents are used to compute the diffusion (q<sub>d</sub>) and capacitive (q<sub>s</sub>) contribution based on the following equation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e1342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403321-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b64ee192-129c-48a7-aab2-cdec03897a22/smll202403321-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403321:smll202403321-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403321-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mi>b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i\ = \ a{{v}^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>Where <i>i</i> and <i>ν</i> represent peak current and scan rate, respectively, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the adjustable parameters. The peaks are denoted as A1, A2, and O1 in the CV plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0005">5b</a>). Generally, if the <i>b</i> value is 0.5 then the reaction is a pure diffusion-controlled (q<sub>d</sub>), and <i>b</i> = 1.0 represents an ideal capacitive controlled reaction (q<sub>s</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e1385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For WS<sub>2</sub>, the <i>b</i> values are (<i>b</i>A1 = 0.52), (<i>b</i>A2 = 0.84), and (<i>b</i>O1 = 0.79), which represents the contribution comes from mainly diffusion controlled but there is the combination of both diffusion and surface control reaction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0005">5c</a>).</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e6f3c3ae-01b9-4b42-adfb-7d6da700b14f/smll202403321-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e6f3c3ae-01b9-4b42-adfb-7d6da700b14f/smll202403321-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/178884bf-756b-40a3-8ba1-d1cfc8b38514/smll202403321-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e6f3c3ae-01b9-4b42-adfb-7d6da700b14f/smll202403321-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower at different scan rates, b) <i>b</i>-Values determination from cathodic and anodic peaks at various scan rates, c) capacity contribution (diffusion and surface controlled) at different scan rates.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">Further, contributions from diffusion and surface control were measured using the following equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0009_R_d198278662e1431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403321-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f50889dd-310e-418c-9a87-2b6f0b3e25a2/smll202403321-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403321:smll202403321-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403321-math-0005.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi>V</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i\ \left( V \right) = {{k}_1}\ v + {{k}_2}{{v}^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The current response, <i>i</i>, at specific voltage, <i>V</i>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are constants and <i>ν</i> is the sweep rate. The areas of CV curves increase with increasing scan rates; in the meantime, diffusion contribution decreases, and the capacitive contribution increases, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0005">5c</a>. A larger capacitive contribution means faster Na<sup>+</sup>-ion transportation, resulting in better cyclic stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0050">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="smll202403321-sec-0050-title">2.2.1 Electrochemical Characterization as High-Temperature NIB Anode</h4>
               
               <p>Inspired by the room temperature NIB performance of carbon-free WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower, we have performed NIB anode testing at elevated temperature (65&nbsp;°C) at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The GCD profiles at various cycle intervals are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0006">6a</a>, which are very similar to the potential profiles shown in GCD plots at room temperature. The corresponding charge–discharge profile is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0005">5a</a>, wherein the first radiation can observe the peaks at 0.6 and 0.4&nbsp;V because of SEI layer formation, and one more change can be observed near 1.75 is because of the conversion reaction of WS<sub>2</sub> with Na<sup>+</sup> to form Na<sub>x</sub>WS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The de-sodiation profile shows three changes ≈2.1, 1.9, and 1.7&nbsp;V, which align well with the pattern observed in the cyclic voltammogram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0004">4a</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0006">6b</a> represents the cycling stability of the WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (at 65&nbsp;°C). The observed initial specific charge capacity of ≈440&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with the Coulombic efficiency of 71%. After initial decay for couple of cycles, it became stable to ≈180&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> up to 150 continuous cycles at 65&nbsp;°C. These results confirm that the WS<sub>2</sub> would be a suitable choice for next-generation variable temperature NIB anode application. According to our best knowledge, there is no report on WS<sub>2</sub> as high-temperature NIB anode.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd407cc7-00a1-4fe8-a543-8c97539c411b/smll202403321-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd407cc7-00a1-4fe8-a543-8c97539c411b/smll202403321-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6736e373-c3f7-4178-b5aa-2ef7a38494a8/smll202403321-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd407cc7-00a1-4fe8-a543-8c97539c411b/smll202403321-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GCD profiles of different cycle intervals for WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower at high temperature (65&nbsp;°C), and b) corresponding cycling performance and coulombic efficiency at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current rate at 65&nbsp;°C.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403321-sec-0060-title">2.3 Structural Characterization of WS<sub>2</sub> Electrode after Cycling</h3>
            
            <p>XPS and TEM analysis, along with elemental mapping, was conducted to investigate the chemical state and morphological or structural alterations of the electrode material after cycling. Although the micro-flower-like morphology is not clearly visible, The TEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) reveal the undisturbed sheet-like morphology remains intact even after undergoing 500 cycles at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The nanoparticles on the WS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet are mainly activated carbon (added during the electrode fabrication) and decomposed electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0024_R_d198278662e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Nanoparticle Res.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, elemental mapping and EDX analysis confirm the homogeneous distribution of W and S along the entire surface. In addition to W and S, the presence of Na, C, and O is residual excess sodium ions originating during the electrochemical reaction and by-product of electrolyte decomposition (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0007">7a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/76165d27-19bf-4180-aefb-43bb88366110/smll202403321-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/76165d27-19bf-4180-aefb-43bb88366110/smll202403321-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2883a8f1-8af6-4a7b-a9b7-154835cf2c8d/smll202403321-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/76165d27-19bf-4180-aefb-43bb88366110/smll202403321-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) HRTEM and elemental mapping image of WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower electrode after 500 cycles with corresponding elements present b) XPS survey spectrum c) W4f (d) Na1s.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0007">7b</a> illustrates the survey spectrum of the WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower electrode after 500 cycles, and it confirms the presence of tungsten and sodium in the electrode material. Interestingly presence of Na1s binding energy peak suggests the insertion of sodium in the material during the cycling process. High-resolution W spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0007">7c</a>) clearly shows an additional peak when compared with the pristine sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0002">2e</a>). A shifting provides valuable insight into the reaction mechanisms occurring between sodium and WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower. Deconvolution of the W spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0007">7c</a>) shows five binding energy peaks, two sets 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and 4f<sub>5/2</sub>, and W5p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks. The binding energy peaks at higher regions correspond to the WS<sub>2</sub> phase as also observed for the pristine material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0018_R_d198278662e1649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Technol.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the lower binding energy peaks confirm the presence of metallic W along with W with a lower oxidation state which can be formed on cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0025_R_d198278662e1654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">25</a>-<a href="#smll202403321-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0027_R_d198278662e1654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, this result corroborates our proposed charge–discharge mechanism. Further analysis of Na1s core level spectrum in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0007">7d</a> shows additional evidence of sodium interacting with the electrode material. The deconvoluted peaks also show the presence of sodium components formed during charge–discharge reactions and/or from electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0028_R_d198278662e1662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403321-sec-0070-title">2.4 Geometry Optimization for Bulk Crystals of WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub></h3>
            
            <p>We have compared the optimized lattice parameters for both structures using two distinct exchange and correlation functionals, as listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), along with the experimental values. Since the results for both functionals are in good agreement, we are presenting calculations with DFT-D3 due to computational cost. The top and side view of a 3&nbsp;×&nbsp;3 supercell of WS<sub>2</sub> is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8a,b</a>. The lattice parameters and angles obtained from optimization calculations for WS<sub>2</sub> are a = b = 3.16, c = 12.72 A. These values are in good agreement with experiments displaying a deviation of less than 1% for <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> parameters, while the <i>c</i> deviation lies in between 1.5% and 2.0%. At room temperature, WO<sub>3</sub> exhibits monoclinic symmetry, and it is well stablished that triclinic, orthorhombic, tetragonal, and cubic symmetries can also manifest by increasing temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0029_R_d198278662e1701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mol. Struct.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general, orthorhombic symmetry is assigned to the space group <i>Pmnb</i>. However, <i>Pbcm</i> symmetry can be obtained by halving the <i>c</i> lattice parameter.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0029_R_d198278662e1713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mol. Struct.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Orthorhombic <i>Pbcm</i> WO<sub>3</sub> structure is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8c,d</a>. The optimized lattice parameters are: <i>a</i> = 7.50, <i>b</i> = 7.67 and <i>c</i> = 3.90&nbsp;Å, with <i>α</i> = <i>β</i> = γ = 90°, differing in 1.5% and 2% with experimental values. Overall, theoretical results are in good agreement with the experimental characterization techniques.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3c48b809-8d4e-48e9-82f3-946d39fe2ccc/smll202403321-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3c48b809-8d4e-48e9-82f3-946d39fe2ccc/smll202403321-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1d0be869-2d69-4261-bb1a-5832d276f19b/smll202403321-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3c48b809-8d4e-48e9-82f3-946d39fe2ccc/smll202403321-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Top and b) side view of the optimized 3&nbsp;×&nbsp;3 supercell of hexagonal WS<sub>2</sub> highlighting the (002) plane. b) top view of the optimized WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer depicting the selected sites for Na adsorption (black dashed circles) with respective labels. d) Top and e) side views of the optimized 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;1&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 bulk orthorhombic WO<sub>3</sub> cell highlighting the (001) O terminated monolayer. f) Top view of the optimized 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;1&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 supercell of O terminated (001) monolayer of oWO<sub>3</sub> highlighting the selected sites for Na atoms with respective labels.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Following the morphological and structural experimental results, the hierarchical structure of the micro-flowers can be thought of as composed of WS<sub>2</sub> (002) and oWO<sub>3</sub> (001) sheets. Therefore, to provide an atomistic insight into the different aspects of both structures we investigate the electronic properties of WS<sub>2</sub> (002) (referred to as WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8b</a>) and WO<sub>3</sub> (001) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8c</a>) monolayer as well as their interaction with Na ions by calculating the adsorption energies on the selected sites. To further investigate the mobility of Na ions on both nanostructures, we employ the NEB method to calculate the diffusion barrier for Na atoms. For the WO<sub>3</sub> monolayer, we have considered the structure terminated in O on both sides (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8e</a>), labeled as oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0080">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="smll202403321-sec-0080-title">2.4.1 Electronic Structure</h4>
               
               <p>Both bulk WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> display semiconductor behavior, as seen by their electronic band structure, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a,b</a> (Supporting Information). An indirect electronic bandgap of 1.17&nbsp;eV is found for WS<sub>2</sub>, with the Valence Band Maximum (VBM) located at Γ and the Conduction Band Minimum (CBM) located in between points K and Γ (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information) in good agreement with previous studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0030_R_d198278662e1813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Phys. Rev. B">30</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0031_R_d198278662e1813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Orthorhombic WO<sub>3</sub> displays an indirect bandgap of ≈1.5&nbsp;eV, with VBM and CBM located at point R and Γ respectively, also in agreement with previous studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0029_R_d198278662e1820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mol. Struct.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electronic properties of the WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer and oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O are now discussed in terms of the projected density of states (pDOS) projected into the s, p and d orbitals of W and into the s and p orbitals of S (O) atoms for both nanostructures as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403321-fig-0009">9a,b</a>. Both WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer and oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O exhibits an increased bandgap when compared to their bulk counterparts. The former displays an electronic bandgap of 1.9&nbsp;eV (compared to 1.17&nbsp;eV for bulk WS<sub>2</sub>). The symmetry between up and down electronic states indicate the absence of a magnetic moment in the structure. The valence bands are dominated by W d states followed by S p states with an indicative of strong hybridization at 1&nbsp;eV below the Fermi energy, as seen by the superposition of the peaks for both states. For oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O, the electronic bandgap is found to be roughly 2.9&nbsp;eV (compared to 1.5&nbsp;eV for the bulk oWO<sub>3</sub>). The small asymmetry between spin up and down electronic states indicate the presence of a non-zero magnetic moment on the nanostructure, as expected by the presence of the top and bottom O undercoordinated atoms. These results are further confirmed by the final magnetic moment at the top and bottom O atoms (highlighted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0009">9b</a> inset) whose value is found to be <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/db8f98cb-dac2-4402-9aa7-e164234e4799/smll202403321-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403321:smll202403321-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202403321-math-0006.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>μ</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                           <mo>=</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                           <mn>1.47</mn>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>μ</mi>
                              <mi>B</mi>
                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\mu \; = \;1.47{{\mu }_B}$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math>. In contrast with WS<sub>2</sub>, valence bands in oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O are found to be dominated by O p states, with little contribution of W d states. For the conduction bands, the opposite trend is observed: these are dominated by W d states followed by O p states, in this case similar to the sulfide counterpart.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="smll202403321-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/46eb0af4-3cc9-4169-a69f-9a52de341c44/smll202403321-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/46eb0af4-3cc9-4169-a69f-9a52de341c44/smll202403321-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c7a09941-30e9-418b-a28a-89f56730fbe9/smll202403321-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/46eb0af4-3cc9-4169-a69f-9a52de341c44/smll202403321-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403321-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403321" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Projected Density of States (pDOS) for a) WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer and b) oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O projected into the s and p states of O (S) atoms and s, p, and d states of W.                      </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0090">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="smll202403321-sec-0090-title">2.4.2 Na Adsorption and Diffusion Barrier</h4>
               
               <p>We now discuss the interaction of Na ions with both surfaces by considering the adsorption and diffusion process of Na atoms on the WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer and in oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O. For WS<sub>2</sub>, we have considered three possible sites for adsorption: i) on top of the W atom (Top<sub>W</sub> site), ii) on top of the S atom (Top<sub>S</sub>), and iii) on the Hollow site (Hollow) as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8c</a>. For oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O, we have considered i) the adsorption on top of the highest O atom (Top<sub>O1</sub>), ii) adsorption on top of the O atom bridging two neighboring W (Top<sub>O2</sub>) and iii) the hollow site in the middle of the square formed by 4&nbsp;W atoms, represented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0008">8f</a>.</p>
               
               <p>The results for the adsorption energy in both nanostructures are shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202403321-tbl-0001">1</a>. It is possible to verify that the interaction of Na ions with the WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer is much weaker when compared to the results for oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O, as indicated by the positive adsorption energies for all the three sites considered. Na adsorption is more likely to occur on Top<sub>S</sub> or at the Hollow site, indicated by the smaller adsorption energies of 0.465 and 0.460&nbsp;eV, respectively. On the other hand, oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O shows a strong interaction with adsorption energies of −1.963, −2.334, and −1.099&nbsp;eV for the TopO1, TopO2, and Hollow sites, respectively. In this case, Na adsorption is likely to occur in all sites. However, the mobility will be reduced as the adsorption interaction increases. Therefore, Na atom mobility will probably occur from the Hollow site. Comparing both nanostructures, it is possible to infer that the mobility of Na ions in oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O should be reduced when compared to WS<sub>2</sub>. The origin of the strong adsorption on the former could be related to the presence of uncoordinated O atoms on both sides of the nanostructure, which could act as reactive sites to bind the Na ions strongly hindering its mobility. Since both W and S atoms are saturated in the WS<sub>2</sub> (that is, they retain the same coordination as in the bulk), the interaction with Na atoms could be mainly attributed to vdW forces, therefore explaining the weaker interaction and higher mobility.</p>
               
               <div class="article-table-content" id="smll202403321-tbl-0001">
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  1.

                                 <p>EADS [EV]</p></th>
                              </tr>
                           </thead>
