                  </div>
                  
                  
                  <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
               </div>
               
               <p>We now analyze the kinetic aspect of Na mobility on WS<sub>2</sub> and oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O nanostructures by calculating the energy barrier with NEB method. For the WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer, we have considered as initial and final states the sites with more probability to bind a Na atom, that is the Hollow sites. On the other hand, since all three sites selected on oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O can bind Na atoms strongly, we selected two adjacent Hollow sites due to the weaker interaction with Na (when compared to Top<sub>O1</sub> and Top<sub>O2</sub> sites). The results for the NEB calculations and the intermediate images on the MEP are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403321-fig-0009">9c,d</a>, respectively. For WS<sub>2</sub>, we found a low energy barrier for the diffusion of Na between two neighboring Hollow sites (0.05&nbsp;eV), indicating the higher mobility in this structure. On the other hand, the energy barrier for oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O is found to be 0.5&nbsp;eV, much higher in comparison with WS<sub>2</sub> confirming the reduced mobility in accordance with both adsorption calculations and electrochemical experiments.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202403321-sec-0100">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202403321-sec-0100-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this groundbreaking study, we investigate and compare the sodium storage performance of WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> for the first time, utilizing experimental and computational approaches. The hierarchical micro-flower structure of WS<sub>2</sub>, composed of collectively assembled nanosheets ranging from 1.0 to 1.5&nbsp;µm in diameter, offers a significant surface area conducive to ion passage. Interconnected nanosheets create abundant ionic channels within the electrolyte, enabling extensive internal surfaces for electrochemical redox reactions. The carbon-free WS<sub>2</sub> micro-flower exhibits a notable specific capacity of ≈334 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, nearly three times higher than its oxide counterpart. DFT calculations shows that nanostructuring increases the electronic bandgap from 1.17 (bulk WS<sub>2</sub>) to 1.9&nbsp;eV (WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer) and from 1.5 (bulk oWO<sub>3</sub>) to 2.9&nbsp;eV (oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O). Furthermore, Na is found to bind strongly in oWO<sub>3</sub>-001-O, with <i>E<sub>ads</sub></i> &lt; −1&nbsp;eV. The origin of such strong interaction is attributed to the presence of undercoordinated O atoms, resulting in an energy diffusion barrier of 0.5&nbsp;eV much higher in comparison to the one found for WS<sub>2</sub> monolayer (0.05&nbsp;eV), where both W and S atoms retain the same coordination as in the bulk structure. This experimental and theoretical evidence lays the foundation for future advancements in high-energy density sodium-ion anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202403321-sec-0110">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202403321-sec-0110-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0120">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0130">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202403321-sec-0140-title"> Synthesis of WO<sub>3</sub> Microflower</h4>
                  
                  <p>In the methodology employed, tungsten hexachloride (WCl<sub>6</sub>, 99%) and thioacetamide (TAA, ACS reagent, ≥99.0%) were procured from Alfa Aesar. A one-pot hydrothermal synthesis was conducted using double distilled water. Initially, 1.6&nbsp;g of WCl<sub>6</sub> and 1.26&nbsp;g of CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub> were combined in 40&nbsp;mL of double distilled water following 30&nbsp;min of sonication and an additional hour of stirring. The resulting reaction mixture was then transferred to a Teflon-lined 50&nbsp;mL autoclave and subjected to heating in a hot air oven at 180&nbsp;°C for 48&nbsp;h. Subsequent to cooling, the resulting grey-colored precipitate underwent washing via centrifugation with deionized water and ethanol. It was then dried for 24&nbsp;h under vacuum at 80&nbsp;°C. The obtained sample underwent an annealing process at 700&nbsp;°C to achieve phase-pure WO<sub>3</sub>, which was subsequently subjected to both structural and electrochemical characterization.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202403321-sec-0150-title"> Synthesis of WS<sub>2</sub> Micro-flower</h4>
                  
                  <p>The synthesis process remained consistent until the completion of the hydrothermal synthesis, leading to the acquisition of the grey-colored precipitate. The subsequent step involved the introduction of CS<sub>2</sub> gas through a tube furnace within an argon environment. This process was crucial for the proper sulfurization of the dried sample at 850&nbsp;°C, resulting in the production of a phase-pure WS<sub>2</sub> sample. This WS<sub>2</sub> sample underwent further structural and electrochemical characterization.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202403321-sec-0160-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The sample's morphological studies were conducted using a field-emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, Thermoscientific Apreo S). Analysis of phase and crystal structure was performed through X-ray diffraction patterns obtained using a diffractometer (XRD, BRUKER USA D8 Advance, Davinci). Surface chemical states were examined via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Physical Electronics). Additionally, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM, JEOL Japan, JEM-2100 Plus) was employed to investigate the morphology and crystal structure at the nanoscale, utilizing high-resolution imaging and selected area electron diffraction patterns (SAED).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202403321-sec-0170-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The investigation of the electrochemical sodium storage property involved the assembly of 2032-type coin cells (MTI-USA). The standard slurry casting technique was employed to fabricate the working electrodes. A slurry was prepared by blending the active material, super-P carbon, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder in a weight ratio of 70:20:10 using n-methyl pyrrolidinone (N.M.P.) solvent, utilizing a mixer (Thinky). This slurry was coated onto a copper foil (15&nbsp;µm thickness) and dried for 12&nbsp;h at 80&nbsp;°C in a vacuum oven. The coated copper foil underwent calendaring at a pressure of 5.0 tons per square inch, shaping the electrodes into 12.5&nbsp;mm diameter disks, with an active material loading of ≈1.7&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The assembly of coin cells was conducted inside a glove box filled with Ar (M'BRAUN, Germany), maintaining moisture and oxygen levels below &lt;0.5&nbsp;ppm. Shiny sodium metal was utilized in fabricating the NIB half-cells as the counter electrode and reference electrode. Whatman paper served as the separator, while the electrolyte consisted of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme (Thermo Fisher Scientific, purity 99+%). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were conducted using a galvanostat potentiostat (PGSTAT30, Autolab, The Netherlands) at various scan rates. Galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements were performed using a LAND CT2001A battery tester. Additionally, high-temperature battery testing was carried out by housing the cell inside an oven.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403321-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202403321-sec-0180-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>State-of-art ab-initio calculations based on Density Functional Theory (DFT) were carried out using the Quantum Espresso computational package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0032_R_d198278662e2214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Phys. Condens. Matter.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The core electrons were treated with the frozen core approximation employing the pseudopotentials method using the well-established files from the Standard Solid State Pseudopotentials (SSSP) library.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0033_R_d198278662e2219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - npj Comput. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange and correlation function was approximated by the Perdew, Burke, and Ernzerhof parametrization of the generalized gradient approximation (GGA-PBE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0034_R_d198278662e2224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Long-range interactions were considered by comparing two distinct functionals: the rVV10.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0035_R_d198278662e2230" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Phys. Rev. B – Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the vdW-DF3.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0036_R_d198278662e2235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Chem. Phys.">36</a>, <a href="#smll202403321-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0037_R_d198278662e2235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Chem. Theory Comput.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Brillouin zone sampling was performed employing a uniform k-point grid within the Monkhorst-Pack scheme.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0038_R_d198278662e2241" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Phys. Rev. B">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the bulk structures, a mesh of (2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2) and (8&nbsp;×&nbsp;8&nbsp;×&nbsp;8) k-points was employed for geometry optimization and electronic structure calculations, respectively. In the case of the monolayers, a (2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;×&nbsp;1) and (8&nbsp;×&nbsp;8&nbsp;×&nbsp;1) k-points mesh was used. Kohn–Sham orbitals were expanded using a plane wave basis set imposing a wavefunction kinetic energy and electronic density cutoff of 50 and 400&nbsp;Ry for WS<sub>2</sub> and 60 and 580&nbsp;Ry for WO<sub>3</sub> as suggested by the SSSP library. The convergence criteria for self-consistency were set to 10<sup>−7</sup> Ry. In geometry optimization calculations, the energy and forces were minimized until the difference in two consecutive ionic steps were within 10<sup>−4</sup> Ry and 10<sup>−3</sup> Ry&nbsp;Bohr<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
                  
                  <p>In order to correctly describe the localization of 5d electrons in W atoms, the Hubbard correction has been applied in all the calculations using the DFT+U approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0039_R_d198278662e2262" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Phys. Rev. B">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For WS<sub>2</sub>, the value of UW = 3.15&nbsp;eV was used, whereas for WO<sub>3</sub>, W 5d, and O 2p electrons were corrected by the values of U<sub>W</sub> = 7.0 and U<sub>O</sub> = 8.0&nbsp;eV, respectively. These parameters were close to the ones used in electronic structure calculations for monoclinic and hexagonal WO<sub>3</sub> reported by Hurtado–Aular.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0040_R_d198278662e2278" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Sur. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and gave a good estimation of the electronic bandgap for orthorhombic WO<sub>3</sub>.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">To investigate the interaction of WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub> nanostructures with Na ions, it had calculated the adsorption energies using the following equations:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403321-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/985e8d4c-5f2c-4ebf-b9b3-9bf364666c44/smll202403321-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403321:smll202403321-math-0007" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403321-math-0007.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>a</mi>
                                          <mi>d</mi>
                                          <mi>s</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>W</mi>
                                          <mi>X</mi>
                                          <mo>+</mo>
                                          <mi>N</mi>
                                          <mi>a</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>W</mi>
                                                <mi>X</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mo>+</mo>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>N</mi>
                                                <mi>a</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfenced>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{E}_{ads}} = {{E}_{WX + Na}} - \left( {{{E}_{WX}} + {{E}_{Na}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>Where <i>E</i><sub><i>WX</i> + <i>Na</i></sub> is the energy of the optimized nanostructure with one Na atom adsorbed, <i>E<sub>WX</sub></i> is the energy of the optimized WS<sub>2</sub> or WO<sub>3</sub> nanostructure and <i>E<sub>Na</sub></i> is the energy of one Na atom.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0041_R_d198278662e2351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Mater. Chem. Phys.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> taken as the energy per atom in Sodium (BCC) bulk structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0042_R_d198278662e2357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Mol. Syst. Des. Eng.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further explore the kinetic aspects involved in the Na ion mobility on both nanostructures, it had calculated the diffusion barrier using the Nudged Elastic Band (NEB).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403321-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403321-bib-0043_R_d198278662e2362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> method as implemented in Quantum Espresso. Due to the different interactions of Na atoms with WS<sub>2</sub> and WO<sub>3</sub>, it had selected as initial and final states the sites where both adsorption and mobility were likely used as the criteria for the adsorption energy, as discussed in detail in the following sections. For all NEB calculations, it had considered seven images along the Minimum Energy Path (MEP).</div>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

         <ol></ol>
      </div>
